<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover,_(10118).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover%2C_%2810118%29.jpg/220px-2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover%2C_%2810118%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover%2C_%2810118%29.jpg/330px-2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover%2C_%2810118%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover%2C_%2810118%29.jpg/440px-2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover%2C_%2810118%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2016-04-23_Anti-TTIP-Demonstration_in_Hannover,_(10118).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anti-<a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_Trade_and_Investment_Partnership" title="Transatlantic Trade and Investment Partnership">TTIP</a> demonstration in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hannover" title="Hannover">Hannover</a>, Germany, 2016</div></div></div>
<p>Anti-globalization, or counter-globalization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> consists of a number of criticisms of globalization but, in general, is critical of the globalization of <a href="/wiki/Corporate_capitalism" title="Corporate capitalism">corporate capitalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The movement is also commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Alter-globalization" title="Alter-globalization">alter-globalization</a> movement, anti-globalist movement, <a href="/wiki/Anti-corporate_activism" title="Anti-corporate activism">anti-corporate</a> globalization movement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Juris_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Juris-190">[190]</a></sup> or movement against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoliberal" title="Neoliberal">neoliberal</a> globalization. Opponents of globalization argue that power and respect in terms of international trade between the developed and underdeveloped countries of the world are unequally distributed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> The diverse subgroups that make up this movement include some of the following: trade unionists, environmentalists, anarchists, land rights and indigenous rights activists, organizations promoting human rights and sustainable development, opponents of privatization, and <a href="/wiki/Anti-sweatshop_movement" title="Anti-sweatshop movement">anti-sweatshop</a> campaigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stwr.org_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-stwr.org-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>The Revolt of the Elites and the Betrayal of Democracy</i>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Lasch" title="Christopher Lasch">Christopher Lasch</a> analyzes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> the widening gap between the top and bottom of the social composition in the United States. For him, our epoch is determined by a social phenomenon: the revolt of the elites, in reference to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Revolt_of_the_Masses" title="The Revolt of the Masses">The Revolt of the Masses</a></i> (1929) by the Spanish philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ortega_y_Gasset" title="José Ortega y Gasset">José Ortega y Gasset</a>. According to Lasch, the new elites, i.e. those who are in the top 20% in terms of income, through globalization which allows total mobility of capital, no longer live in the same world as their fellow-citizens. In this, they oppose the old bourgeoisie of the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, which was constrained by its spatial stability to a minimum of rooting and civic obligations. Globalization, according to the sociologist, has turned elites into tourists in their own countries. The denationalization of business enterprise tends to produce a class who see themselves as "world citizens, but without accepting ... any of the obligations that citizenship in a polity normally implies". Their ties to an international culture of work, leisure, information – make many of them deeply indifferent to the prospect of national decline. Instead of financing public services and the public treasury, new elites are investing their money in improving their voluntary ghettos: private schools in their residential neighborhoods, private police, garbage collection systems. They have "withdrawn from common life".
Composed of those who control the international flows of capital and information, who preside over philanthropic foundations and institutions of higher education, manage the instruments of cultural production and thus fix the terms of public debate. So, the political debate is limited mainly to the dominant classes and political ideologies lose all contact with the concerns of the ordinary citizen. The result of this is that no one has a likely solution to these problems and that there are furious ideological battles on related issues.
However, they remain protected from the problems affecting the working classes: the decline of industrial activity, the resulting loss of employment, the decline of the middle class, increasing the number of the poor, the rising crime rate, growing drug trafficking, the urban crisis.
</p><p>D.A. Snow et al. contend that the <a href="/wiki/Anti-globalization_movement" title="Anti-globalization movement">anti-globalization movement</a> is an example of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_social_movement" title="New social movement">new social movement</a>, which uses tactics that are unique and use different resources than previously used before in other social movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most infamous tactics of the movement is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Seattle_(1999)" title="Battle of Seattle (1999)">Battle of Seattle</a> in 1999, where there were protests against the World Trade Organization's Third Ministerial Meeting. All over the world, the movement has held protests outside meetings of institutions such as the WTO, the International Monetary Fund (IMF), the World Bank, the World Economic Forum, and the Group of Eight (G8).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stwr.org_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-stwr.org-192">[192]</a></sup> Within the Seattle demonstrations the protesters that participated used both creative and violent tactics to gain the attention towards the issue of globalization.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_capital_market_integration">Opposition to capital market integration</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-capitalist_movements" title="Anti-capitalist movements">Anti-capitalist movements</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg/220px-Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg/330px-Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg/440px-Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worldbank_protest_jakarta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World Bank Protester, <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, Indonesia</div></div></div>
<p>Capital markets have to do with raising and investing money in various human enterprises. Increasing integration of these <a href="/wiki/Financial_market" title="Financial market">financial markets</a> between countries leads to the emergence of a global capital marketplace or a single world market. In the long run, increased movement of capital between countries tends to favor owners of capital more than any other group; in the short run, owners and workers in specific sectors in capital-exporting countries bear much of the burden of adjusting to increased movement of capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Those opposed to capital market integration on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> issues are especially disturbed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (March 2020)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> by the various abuses which they think are perpetuated by global and international institutions that, they say, promote <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a> without regard to ethical standards. Common targets include the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> (WB), <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> (OECD) and the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a> (WTO) and <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> treaties like the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Agreement</a> (NAFTA), <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Area_of_the_Americas" title="Free Trade Area of the Americas">Free Trade Area of the Americas</a> (FTAA), the <a href="/wiki/Multilateral_Agreement_on_Investment" title="Multilateral Agreement on Investment">Multilateral Agreement on Investment</a> (MAI) and the <a href="/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Trade_in_Services" title="General Agreement on Trade in Services">General Agreement on Trade in Services</a> (GATS). In light of the economic gap between rich and poor countries, movement adherents claim free trade without measures in place to protect the under-capitalized will contribute only to the strengthening the power of industrialized nations (often termed the "North" in opposition to the developing world's "South").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (March 2019)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-corporatism_and_anti-consumerism">Anti-corporatism and anti-consumerism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-corporatism" title="Anti-corporatism">Anti-corporatism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-consumerism" title="Anti-consumerism">Anti-consumerism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">Corporatist</a> ideology, which privileges the rights of corporations (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_personality" title="Legal personality">artificial or juridical persons</a>) over those of <a href="/wiki/Natural_person" title="Natural person">natural persons</a>, is an underlying factor in the recent rapid expansion of global commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> In recent years, there have been an increasing number of books (<a href="/wiki/Naomi_Klein" title="Naomi Klein">Naomi Klein</a>'s 2000 <i><a href="/wiki/No_Logo" title="No Logo">No Logo</a></i>, for example) and films (<i>e.g. <a href="/wiki/The_Corporation_(2003_film)" title="The Corporation (2003 film)">The Corporation</a></i> &amp; <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surplus_(film)" title="Surplus (film)">Surplus</a></i>) popularizing an <a href="/wiki/Anti-corporate_activism" title="Anti-corporate activism">anti-corporate</a> <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> to the public.
</p><p>A related contemporary ideology, <a href="/wiki/Consumerism" title="Consumerism">consumerism</a>, which encourages the personal acquisition of goods and services, also drives globalization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Anti-consumerism is a social movement against equating personal happiness with consumption and the purchase of material possessions. Concern over the treatment of consumers by large corporations has spawned substantial activism, and the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Consumer_education" title="Consumer education">consumer education</a> into school <a href="/wiki/Curriculum" title="Curriculum">curricula</a>. Social activists hold materialism is connected to <a href="/wiki/Big-box_store" title="Big-box store">global retail merchandizing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supplier_convergence" title="Supplier convergence">supplier convergence</a>, <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>, greed, <a href="/wiki/Anomie" title="Anomie">anomie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">crime</a>, environmental degradation, and general social <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">malaise</a> and discontent. One variation on this topic is activism by <i>postconsumers</i>, with the strategic emphasis on moving <i>beyond</i> addictive consumerism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_justice_and_inequality">Global justice and inequality</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_justice">Global justice</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Global_justice_movement" title="Global justice movement">Global justice movement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3354" data-file-width="6300" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png/220px-GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png/330px-GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png/440px-GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Differences in national income equality around the world as measured by the national <a href="/wiki/Gini_coefficient" title="Gini coefficient">Gini coefficient</a>, as of 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The global justice movement is the loose collection of individuals and groups—often referred to as a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Movement_of_movements" title="Movement of movements">movement of movements</a>"—who advocate <a href="/wiki/Fair_trade" title="Fair trade">fair trade</a> rules and perceive current institutions of global economic integration as problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The movement is often labeled an anti-globalization movement by the mainstream media. Those involved, however, frequently deny that they are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-globalization" title="Anti-globalization">anti-globalization</a>, insisting that they support the globalization of communication and people and oppose only the global expansion of corporate power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> The movement is based in the idea of <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>, desiring the creation of a society or institution based on the principles of <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">equality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solidarity" title="Solidarity">solidarity</a>, the values of human rights, and the dignity of every human being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2006_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2006-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2005_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2005-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structural_inequality" title="Structural inequality">Social inequality</a> within and between nations, including a growing <a href="/wiki/Global_digital_divide" title="Global digital divide">global digital divide</a>, is a focal point of the movement. Many nongovernmental organizations have now arisen to fight these inequalities that many in Latin America, Africa and Asia face. A few very popular and well known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organizations" title="Non-governmental organizations">non-governmental organizations</a> (NGOs) include: <a href="/wiki/War_Child_(charity)" title="War Child (charity)">War Child</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Cross" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_The_Children" title="Free The Children">Free The Children</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CARE_International" title="CARE International">CARE International</a>. They often create partnerships where they work towards improving the lives of those who live in developing countries by building schools, fixing infrastructure, cleaning water supplies, purchasing equipment and supplies for hospitals, and other aid efforts.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_total_wealth_(trillions_USD),_Credit_Suisse.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3365" data-file-width="7192" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Countries_by_total_wealth_%28trillions_USD%29%2C_Credit_Suisse.png/260px-Countries_by_total_wealth_%28trillions_USD%29%2C_Credit_Suisse.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Countries_by_total_wealth_%28trillions_USD%29%2C_Credit_Suisse.png/390px-Countries_by_total_wealth_%28trillions_USD%29%2C_Credit_Suisse.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Countries_by_total_wealth_%28trillions_USD%29%2C_Credit_Suisse.png/520px-Countries_by_total_wealth_%28trillions_USD%29%2C_Credit_Suisse.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_total_wealth_(trillions_USD),_Credit_Suisse.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_total_wealth" title="List of countries by total wealth">total wealth</a> (trillions USD), <a href="/wiki/Credit_Suisse" title="Credit Suisse">Credit Suisse</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_inequality">Social inequality</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">Social inequality</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_inequality" title="International inequality">International inequality</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="351" data-file-width="735" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png/260px-Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png/390px-Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png/520px-Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global-share-of-wealth-by-wealth-group-768x409.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global share of wealth by wealth group, Credit Suisse, 2017</div></div></div>
<p>The economies of the world have <a href="/wiki/Development_theory" title="Development theory">developed</a> unevenly, historically, such that entire geographical regions were left mired in poverty and disease while others began to reduce poverty and disease on a wholesale basis. From around 1980 through at least 2011, the GDP gap, while still wide, appeared to be closing and, in some more rapidly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_expectancies" title="Life expectancies">life expectancies</a> began to rise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> If we look at the Gini coefficient for world income, since the late 1980s, the gap between some regions has markedly narrowed—between Asia and the advanced economies of the West, for example—but huge gaps remain globally. Overall equality across humanity, considered as individuals, has improved very little. Within the decade between 2003 and 2013, income inequality grew even in traditionally egalitarian countries like Germany, Sweden and Denmark. With a few exceptions—France, Japan, Spain—the top 10 percent of earners in most advanced economies raced ahead, while the bottom 10 percent fell further behind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stiglitz_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stiglitz-207">[207]</a></sup> By 2013, 85 multibillionaires had amassed wealth equivalent to all the wealth owned by the poorest half (3.5 billion) of the world's total population of 7 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WEF2014_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-WEF2014-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics of globalization argue that globalization results in weak <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">labor unions</a>: the surplus in cheap labor coupled with an ever-growing number of companies in transition weakened labor unions in high-cost areas. Unions become less effective and workers their enthusiasm for unions when membership begins to decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurst_E._Charles_P.41_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hurst_E._Charles_P.41-175">[175]</a></sup> They also cite an increase in the exploitation of <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labor</a>: countries with weak protections for children are vulnerable to infestation by rogue companies and criminal gangs who exploit them. Examples include <a href="/wiki/Quarry" title="Quarry">quarrying</a>, salvage, and farm work as well as trafficking, bondage, forced labor, prostitution and pornography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edmonds_Pavcni_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds_Pavcni-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg/170px-May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg/255px-May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg/340px-May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:May_Day_Immigration_March_LA60.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Immigrant rights march for amnesty, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, on <a href="/wiki/International_Workers%27_Day" title="International Workers' Day">May Day</a>, 2006</div></div></div>
<p>Women often participate in the workforce in <a href="/wiki/Precarious_work" title="Precarious work">precarious work</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Export-oriented_employment" title="Export-oriented employment">export-oriented employment</a>. Evidence suggests that while globalization has expanded women's access to employment, the long-term goal of transforming <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">gender inequalities</a> remains unmet and appears unattainable without regulation of capital and a reorientation and expansion of the state's role in funding public goods and providing a social safety net.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Furthermore, the intersectionality of gender, race, class, and more remain overlooked when assessing the impact of globalization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, a study published by the IMF posited that <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a>, the ideological backbone of contemporary globalized capitalism, has been "oversold", with the benefits of neoliberal policies being "fairly difficult to establish when looking at a broad group of countries" and the costs, most significantly higher income inequality within nations, "hurt the level and sustainability of growth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-global_governance">Anti-global governance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Global_governance" title="Global governance">Global governance</a></div>
<p>Beginning in the 1930s, opposition arose to the idea of a world government, as advocated by organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Federalist_Movement" title="World Federalist Movement">World Federalist Movement</a> (WFM). Those who oppose global governance typically do so on objections that the idea is unfeasible, inevitably oppressive, or simply unnecessary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> In general, these opponents are wary of the concentration of power or wealth that such governance might represent. Such reasoning dates back to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> and, later, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmentalist_opposition">Environmentalist opposition</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg/220px-Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg/330px-Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg/440px-Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_highland_plateau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> Highland Plateau has led to extensive <a href="/wiki/Siltation" title="Siltation">siltation</a> and unstable flows of western <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1304" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp/220px-Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp/330px-Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp/440px-Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_footprint_hotspots_of_foreign_final_consumption_in_China.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>a</b> shows carbon footprint (CF) hotspots of foreign final consumption in China. <b>b</b>–<b>d</b> show carbon footprint hotspots of the consumption of the United States, Hong Kong, and Japan, respectively. Among all foreign regions, the United States, Hong Kong, and Japan have the largest CFs in China, contributing ~23.0%, 10.8%, and 9.0%, respectively, to the total foreign CF in China in 2012.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">Environmentalism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">Environmentalism</a> is a broad philosophy, ideology<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> and social movement regarding concerns for environmental <a href="/wiki/Conservation_movement" title="Conservation movement">conservation</a> and improvement of the health of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>. Environmentalist concerns with globalization include issues such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, global <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_crisis" title="Water crisis">water crises</a>, inequity in <a href="/wiki/Energy_consumption" title="Energy consumption">energy consumption</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">energy conservation</a>, transnational <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> and pollution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_ocean" title="World ocean">world ocean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, world <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitat</a> <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">species extinction</a>.
</p><p>One critique of globalization is that natural resources of the poor have been systematically taken over by the rich and the pollution promulgated by the rich is systematically dumped on the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Some argue that Northern corporations are increasingly exploiting resources of less wealthy countries for their global activities while it is the South that is disproportionately bearing the environmental burden of the globalized economy. Globalization is thus leading to a type of" environmental <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Helena_Norberg-Hodge" title="Helena Norberg-Hodge">Helena Norberg-Hodge</a>, the director and founder of Local Futures/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Ecology_and_Culture" title="International Society for Ecology and Culture">International Society for Ecology and Culture</a>, criticizes globalization in many ways. In her book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Futures:_Learning_from_Ladakh" title="Ancient Futures: Learning from Ladakh">Ancient Futures</a></i>, Norberg-Hodge claims that "centuries of ecological balance and social harmony are under threat from the pressures of development and globalization." She also criticizes the standardization and rationalization of globalization, as it does not always yield the expected growth outcomes. Although globalization takes similar steps in most countries, scholars such as Hodge claim that it might not be effective to certain countries and that globalization has actually moved some countries backward instead of developing them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>A related area of concern is the <a href="/wiki/Pollution_haven_hypothesis" title="Pollution haven hypothesis">pollution haven hypothesis</a>, which posits that, when large industrialized nations seek to set up factories or offices abroad, they will often look for the cheapest option in terms of resources and labor that offers the land and material access they require (see <a href="/wiki/Race_to_the_bottom" title="Race to the bottom">Race to the bottom</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Unmaskingthe_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Unmaskingthe-220">[220]</a></sup> This often comes at the cost of environmentally sound practices. Developing countries with cheap resources and labor tend to have less stringent <a href="/wiki/Environmental_law" title="Environmental law">environmental regulations</a>, and conversely, nations with stricter environmental regulations become more expensive for companies as a result of the costs associated with meeting these standards. Thus, companies that choose to physically invest in foreign countries tend to (re)locate to the countries with the lowest <a href="/wiki/Environmental_standard" title="Environmental standard">environmental standards</a> or weakest enforcement.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Union%E2%80%93Mercosur_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="European Union–Mercosur Free Trade Agreement">European Union–Mercosur Free Trade Agreement</a>, which would form one of the world's largest free trade areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> has been denounced by <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental activists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Brazil" title="Indigenous peoples in Brazil">indigenous rights</a> campaigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> The fear is that the deal could lead to more <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon rainforest">deforestation of the Amazon rainforest</a> as it expands market access to Brazilian beef.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_security">Food security</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">Food security</a></div>
<p>Globalization is associated with a more efficient system of food production. This is because crops are grown in countries with optimum growing conditions. This improvement causes an increase in the world's food supply which encourages improved food security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> The political movement 'BREXIT' was considered a step back in globalisation; it has greatly disrupted food chains within the UK, as they import 26% of food produce from the EU.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span></h3>
<p>Norway's limited crop range advocates globalization of food production and availability. The northernmost country in Europe requires trade with other countries to ensure population food demands are met. The degree of self-sufficiency in food production is around 50% in Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span></h2>

"The Global Moment: The Emergence of Globality, 1866–1867, and the Origins of Nineteenth-Century Globalization." <i>The Historian</i> 81.1 (2019): 9. <a class="external text" href="https://www.questia.com/library/journal/1G1-582097534/the-global-moment-the-emergence-of-globality-1866-1867" rel="nofollow">online</a>, focus on trade and Suez Canal</li>
<li>Gunder Frank, Andre, and Robert A. Denemark. <i>ReOrienting the 19th Century: Global Economy in the Continuing Asian Age</i> (Paradigm Publishers, 2013);</li>
<li>Hopkins, A.G., ed. <i>Globalization in World History</i> (Norton, 2003).</li>
<li>Lechner, Frank J., and John Boli, eds. <i>The Globalization Reader</i> (4th ed. Wiley-Blackwell, 2012).</li>
<li>Leibler, Anat. "The Emergence of a Global Economic Order: From Scientific Internationalism to Infrastructural Globalism." in <i>Science, Numbers and Politics</i> (Palgrave Macmillan, Cham, 2019) pp. 121–145 <a class="external text" href="http://www.academia.edu/download/59992228/Science_Numbers_and_Politics20190712-70585-1255i4e.pdf" rel="nofollow">online</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link tagged July 2022">dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup>.</li>

<li>Pfister, Ulrich (2012), <a class="external text" href="http://ieg-ego.eu/en/threads/backgrounds/globalization/ulrich-pfister-globalization?set_language=en&amp;-C=" rel="nofollow">Globalization</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ieg-ego.eu/" rel="nofollow">EGO - European History Online</a>, Mainz: <a class="external text" href="http://www.ieg-mainz.de/likecms/index.php" rel="nofollow">Institute of European History</a>, retrieved: 25 March 2021 (<a class="external text" href="http://nbn-resolving.de/urn:nbn:de:0159-2012060507" rel="nofollow">pdf</a>).</li>

<li>Van Der Bly, Martha C.E. "Globalization: A Triumph of Ambiguity," <i>Current Sociology</i> 53:6 (November 2005), 875–893</li>
<li>Wallerstein, Immanuel. "Globalization or the Age of Transition? A Long-Term View of the Trajectory of the World System," <i>International Sociology</i> 15:2 (June 2000), 251–267.</li></ul>
